The <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> of Serbia as a hereditary monarchy under its own <a href="/wiki/Obrenovi%C4%87_dynasty" title="ObrenoviÄ dynasty">dynasty</a> was acknowledged <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> in 1830.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berend2003_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berend2003-115">[107]</a></sup> In 1821, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greece" title="Ottoman Greece">Greeks</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">declared war</a> on the Sultan. A rebellion that originated in Moldavia as a diversion was followed by the main revolution in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, which, along with the northern part of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Corinth" title="Gulf of Corinth">Gulf of Corinth</a>, became the first parts of the Ottoman Empire to achieve independence (in 1829). In 1830, the French invaded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deylik_of_Algiers" title="Deylik of Algiers">Deylik of Algiers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Algiers_in_1830" title="Invasion of Algiers in 1830">The campaign</a> that took 21 days, resulted in over 5,000 Algerian military casualties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-116">[108]</a></sup> and about 2,600 French ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-116">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup> Before the French invasion the total population of Algeria was most likely between 3,000,000 and 5,000,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup> By 1873, the population of Algeria (excluding several hundred thousand newly arrived French settlers) decreased to a drastic 2,172,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup> In 1831, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali Pasha</a> revolted against Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a> due to the latter's refusal to grant him the governorships of <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Greater Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, which the Sultan had promised him in exchange for sending military assistance to put down the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek revolt</a> (1821â1829) that ultimately ended with the formal <a href="/wiki/London_Protocol_(1830)" title="London Protocol (1830)">independence of Greece</a> in 1830. It was a costly enterprise for Muhammad Ali Pasha, who had lost his fleet at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Navarino" title="Battle of Navarino">Battle of Navarino</a> in 1827. Thus began the first <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Ottoman_War_(1831%E2%80%931833)" title="EgyptianâOttoman War (1831â1833)">EgyptianâOttoman War (1831â1833)</a>, during which the French-trained army of Muhammad Ali Pasha, under the command of his son <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Pasha_of_Egypt" title="Ibrahim Pasha of Egypt">Ibrahim Pasha</a>, defeated the Ottoman Army as it marched into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, reaching the city of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCtahya" title="KÃ¼tahya">KÃ¼tahya</a> within 320Â km (200Â mi) of the capital, Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 95">:â95â</span></sup> In desperation, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a> appealed to the empire's traditional arch-rival Russia for help, asking Emperor <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> to send an expeditionary force to assist him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">:â96â</span></sup> In return for signing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_H%C3%BCnk%C3%A2r_%C4%B0skelesi" title="Treaty of HÃ¼nkÃ¢r Ä°skelesi">Treaty of HÃ¼nkÃ¢r Ä°skelesi</a>, the Russians sent the expeditionary force which deterred Ibrahim Pasha from marching any further towards Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">:â96â</span></sup> Under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_K%C3%BCtahya" title="Convention of KÃ¼tahya">Convention of KÃ¼tahya</a>, signed on 5 May 1833, Muhammad Ali Pasha agreed to abandon his campaign against the Sultan, in exchange for which he was made the <i><a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">vali</a></i> (governor) of the <i><a href="/wiki/Vilayet" title="Vilayet">vilayets</a></i> (provinces) of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripoli,_Lebanon" title="Tripoli, Lebanon">Tripoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> (the latter four comprising modern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>), and given the right to collect taxes in <a href="/wiki/Adana" title="Adana">Adana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">:â96â</span></sup> Had it not been for the Russian intervention, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a> could have faced the risk of being overthrown and Muhammad Ali Pasha could have even become the new Sultan. These events marked the beginning of a recurring pattern where the Sublime Porte needed the help of foreign powers to protect itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-4"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 95â96">:â95â96â</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg/220px-Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg/330px-Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg/440px-Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Si%C3%A8ge_de_l%27Acropoles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The siege of the Acropolis in 1826â1827 during the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1839, the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Sublime Porte</a> attempted to take back what it lost to the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> autonomous, but <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> still Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Eyalet of Egypt</a>, but its forces were initially defeated, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Crisis_of_1840" title="Oriental Crisis of 1840">Oriental Crisis of 1840</a>. Muhammad Ali Pasha had close relations with <a href="/wiki/July_Monarchy" title="July Monarchy">France</a>, and the prospect of him becoming the Sultan of Egypt was widely viewed as putting the entire <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> into the French sphere of influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-5"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">:â96â</span></sup> As the Sublime Porte had proved itself incapable of defeating Muhammad Ali Pasha, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a> provided military assistance, and the second <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Ottoman_War_(1839%E2%80%931841)" title="EgyptianâOttoman War (1839â1841)">EgyptianâOttoman War (1839â1841)</a> ended with Ottoman victory and the restoration of Ottoman suzerainty over <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Egypt Eyalet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effraim_120-6"><a href="#cite_note-Effraim-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">:â96â</span></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-19th century, the Ottoman Empire was called the "<a href="/wiki/Sick_man_of_Europe" title="Sick man of Europe">sick man of Europe</a>". Three suzerain states â the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Serbia" title="Principality of Serbia">Principality of Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a> â moved towards <i>de jure</i> independence during the 1860s and 1870s.
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline_and_modernisation_.281828.E2.80.931908.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_modernisation_(1828â1908)">Decline and modernisation (1828â1908)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Decline of the Ottoman Empire">Decline of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="1405" decoding="async" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg/220px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg/330px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg/440px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_ceremony_of_the_First_Ottoman_Parliament_at_the_Dolmabahce_Palace_in_1876.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Opening ceremony of the First <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Parliament" title="Ottoman Parliament">Ottoman Parliament</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace" title="DolmabahÃ§e Palace">DolmabahÃ§e Palace</a> in 1876. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="First Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">First Constitutional Era</a> lasted only two years until 1878. The Ottoman Constitution and Parliament were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Second Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">restored 30 years later</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a> in 1908.</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> period (1839â1876), the government's series of constitutional reforms led to a fairly modern <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Conscription in the Ottoman Empire">conscripted army</a>, banking system reforms, the decriminalization of homosexuality, the replacement of religious law with secular law<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup> and guilds with modern factories. The Ottoman Ministry of Post was established in Istanbul in 1840. American inventor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Morse" title="Samuel Morse">Samuel Morse</a> received an Ottoman patent for the telegraph in 1847, which was issued by Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_I" title="AbdÃ¼lmecid I">AbdÃ¼lmecid</a> who personally tested the new invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup> The reformist period peaked with the Constitution, called the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kan%C3%BBn-u_Es%C3%A2s%C3%AE" title="KanÃ»n-u EsÃ¢sÃ®">KanÃ»n-u EsÃ¢sÃ®</a></i>. The empire's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="First Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">First Constitutional era</a> was short-lived. The parliament survived for only two years before the sultan suspended it.
</p><p>The Christian population of the empire, owing to their higher educational levels, started to pull ahead of the Muslim majority, leading to much resentment on the part of the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google_b_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-123">[115]</a></sup> In 1861, there were 571 primary and 94 secondary schools for Ottoman Christians with 140,000 pupils in total, a figure that vastly exceeded the number of Muslim children in school at the same time, who were further hindered by the amount of time spent learning Arabic and Islamic theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google_b_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-123">[115]</a></sup> Author Norman Stone further suggests that the Arabic alphabet, in which Turkish was written <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet_reform" title="Turkish alphabet reform">until 1928</a>, was very ill-suited to reflect the sounds of the Turkish language (which is a Turkic as opposed to Semitic language), which imposed a further difficulty on Turkish children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google_b_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-123">[115]</a></sup> In turn, the higher educational levels of the Christians allowed them to play a larger role in the economy, with the rise in prominence of groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Sursock_family" title="Sursock family">Sursock family</a> indicative of this shift in influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google_b_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-123">[115]</a></sup> In 1911, of the 654 wholesale companies in Istanbul, 528 were owned by ethnic Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google_b_123-4"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-123">[115]</a></sup> In many cases, Christians and also Jews were able to gain protection from European consuls and citizenship, meaning they were protected from Ottoman law and not subject to the same economic regulations as their Muslim counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogan201193_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogan201193-125">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="788" data-file-width="1026" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkish_troops_storming_Fort_Shefketil_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman troops storming <a href="/wiki/Shekvetili" title="Shekvetili">Fort Shefketil</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of 1853â1856</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> (1853â1856) was part of a long-running contest between the major European powers for influence over territories of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Decline of the Ottoman Empire">declining Ottoman Empire</a>. The financial burden of the war led the Ottoman state to issue <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_public_debt" title="Ottoman public debt">foreign loans</a> amounting to 5<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million pounds sterling on 4 August 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 32">:â32â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 71">:â71â</span></sup> The war caused an exodus of the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatars" title="Crimean Tatars">Crimean Tatars</a>, about 200,000 of whom moved to the Ottoman Empire in continuing waves of emigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 79â108">:â79â108â</span></sup> Toward the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_Wars" title="Caucasian Wars">Caucasian Wars</a>, 90% of the <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing_of_Circassians" title="Ethnic cleansing of Circassians">ethnically cleansed</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> and exiled from their homelands in the Caucasus and fled to the Ottoman Empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> resulting in the settlement of 500,000 to 700,000 Circassians in Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaimoukha2001_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaimoukha2001-131">[123]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2013)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hille2010_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hille2010-132">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChirotMcCauley2010_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChirotMcCauley2010-133">[125]</a></sup> Some Circassian organisations give much higher numbers, totalling 1â1.5<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million deported or killed. Crimean Tatar refugees in the late 19th century played an especially notable role in seeking to modernise Ottoman education and in first promoting both <a href="/wiki/Pan-Turkism" title="Pan-Turkism">Pan-Turkism</a> and a sense of Turkish nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>In this period, the Ottoman Empire spent only small amounts of public funds on education; for example in 1860â1861 only 0.2 percent of the total budget was invested in education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 50">:â50â</span></sup> As the Ottoman state attempted to modernize its infrastructure and army in response to threats from the outside, it also opened itself up to a different kind of threat: that of creditors. Indeed, as the historian Eugene Rogan has written, "the single greatest threat to the independence of the Middle East" in the nineteenth century "was not the armies of Europe but its banks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogan2011105_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogan2011105-136">[128]</a></sup> The Ottoman state, which had begun taking on debt with the Crimean War, was forced to declare bankruptcy in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogan2011106_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogan2011106-137">[129]</a></sup> By 1881, the Ottoman Empire agreed to have its debt controlled by an institution known as the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Public_Debt_Administration" title="Ottoman Public Debt Administration">Ottoman Public Debt Administration</a>, a council of European men with presidency alternating between France and Britain. The body controlled swaths of the Ottoman economy, and used its position to ensure that European capital continued to penetrate the empire, often to the detriment of local Ottoman interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogan2011106_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogan2011106-137">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ottomans_1875.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5976" data-file-width="5384" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Ottomans_1875.png/220px-Ottomans_1875.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Ottomans_1875.png/330px-Ottomans_1875.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Ottomans_1875.png/440px-Ottomans_1875.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ottomans_1875.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Empire in 1875 under sultan Abdul-Aziz</div></div></div>
<p>The Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Bashi-bazouk" title="Bashi-bazouk">bashi-bazouks</a> brutally suppressed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/April_Uprising" title="April Uprising">Bulgarian uprising</a> of 1876, massacring up to 100,000 people in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 139">:â139â</span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%931878)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877â1878)">Russo-Turkish War (1877â1878)</a> ended with a decisive victory for Russia. As a result, Ottoman holdings in Europe declined sharply: <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Bulgaria" title="Principality of Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> was established as an independent principality inside the Ottoman Empire; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Principalities" title="United Principalities">Romania</a> achieved full independence; and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Serbia" title="History of Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> finally gained complete independence, but with smaller territories. In 1878, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> unilaterally occupied the Ottoman provinces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosnia_Vilayet" title="Bosnia Vilayet">Bosnia-Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Novi_Pazar" title="Sanjak of Novi Pazar">Novi Pazar</a>.
</p><p>British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> advocated for restoring the Ottoman territories on the Balkan Peninsula during the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a>, and in return, Britain assumed the administration of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> in 1878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 228â254">:â228â254â</span></sup> Britain later sent troops to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in 1882 to put down the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urabi_Revolt" title="Urabi Revolt">Urabi Revolt</a> â Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a> was too paranoid to mobilize his own army, fearing this would result in a coup d'Ã©tat â effectively gaining control in both territories. Abdul Hamid II, popularly known as "Abdul Hamid the Damned" on account of his cruelty and paranoia, was so fearful of the threat of a coup that he did not allow his army to conduct war games, lest this serves as the cover for a coup, but he did see the need for military mobilization. In 1883, a German military mission under General Baron <a href="/wiki/Colmar_Freiherr_von_der_Goltz" title="Colmar Freiherr von der Goltz">Colmar von der Goltz</a> arrived to train the Ottoman Army, leading to the so-called "Goltz generation" of German-trained officers who were to play a notable role in the politics of the last years of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 24">:â24â</span></sup>
</p><p>From 1894 to 1896, between 100,000 and 300,000 Armenians living throughout the empire were killed in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">Hamidian massacres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 42">:â42â</span></sup>
</p><p>In 1897 the population was 19<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million, of whom 14<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million (74%) were Muslim. An additional 20<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million lived in provinces that remained under the sultan's nominal suzerainty but were entirely outside his actual power. One by one the Porte lost nominal authority. They included Egypt, Tunisia, Bulgaria, Cyprus, Bosnia-Herzegovina, and Lebanon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the Ottoman Empire gradually shrank in size, some 7â9<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million Muslims from its former territories in the Caucasus, <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, Balkans, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> islands migrated to Anatolia and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Thrace" title="Eastern Thrace">Eastern Thrace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpat2004_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karpat2004-143">[135]</a></sup> After the Empire lost the <a href="/wiki/First_Balkan_War" title="First Balkan War">First Balkan War</a> (1912â1913), it lost all its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan_peninsula" title="Balkan peninsula">Balkan</a> territories except <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a> (European Turkey). This resulted in around 400,000 Muslims fleeing with the retreating Ottoman armies (with many dying from <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> brought by the soldiers), and with some 400,000 non-Muslims fleeing territory still under Ottoman rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Justin_McCarthy_(American_historian)" title="Justin McCarthy (American historian)">Justin McCarthy</a> estimates that during the period 1821 to 1922, 5.5<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million Muslims died in southeastern Europe, with the expulsion of 5<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy1995_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy1995-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmichael2012_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmichael2012-146">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buturovic2010_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buturovic2010-147">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Defeat_and_dissolution_.281908.E2.80.931922.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Defeat_and_dissolution_(1908â1922)">Defeat and dissolution (1908â1922)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ottoman_Empire_during_World_War_I" title="History of the Ottoman Empire during World War I">History of the Ottoman Empire during World War I</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Young_Turk_movement">Young Turk movement</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="587" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png/220px-Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png/330px-Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png/440px-Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_the_1908_Revolution_in_Ottoman_Empire.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Declaration of the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a> by the leaders of the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millets</a> in 1908</div></div></div>
<p>The defeat and <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">dissolution of the Ottoman Empire</a> (1908â1922) began with the <a href="/wiki/Second_Constitutional_Era" title="Second Constitutional Era">Second Constitutional Era</a>, a moment of hope and promise established with the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a>. It restored the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Constitution of the Ottoman Empire">Constitution of the Ottoman Empire</a> and brought in <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of political parties in the Ottoman Empire">multi-party politics</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Elections in the Ottoman Empire">two-stage electoral system</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ottoman_electoral_law" title="Ottoman electoral law">electoral law</a>) under the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="General Assembly of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman parliament</a>. The constitution offered hope by freeing the empire's citizens to modernise the state's institutions, rejuvenate its strength, and enable it to hold its own against outside powers. Its guarantee of liberties promised to dissolve inter-communal tensions and transform the empire into a more harmonious place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (March 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Instead, this period became the story of the twilight struggle of the Empire.
</p><p>Members of <a href="/wiki/Young_Turks" title="Young Turks">Young Turks</a> movement who had once gone underground now established their parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (March 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Among them "<a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Union_and_Progress" title="Committee of Union and Progress">Committee of Union and Progress</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Freedom_and_Accord_Party" title="Freedom and Accord Party">Freedom and Accord Party</a>" were major parties. On the other end of the spectrum were ethnic parties, which included <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party_in_Palestine_(Poale_Zion)" title="Jewish Social Democratic Labour Party in Palestine (Poale Zion)">Poale Zion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Fatat" title="Al-Fatat">Al-Fatat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_national_movement" title="Armenian national movement">Armenian national movement</a> organised under <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Revolutionary_Federation" title="Armenian Revolutionary Federation">Armenian Revolutionary Federation</a>. Profiting from the civil strife, Austria-Hungary officially annexed <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> in 1908. The last of the <a href="/wiki/Census_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Census in the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman censuses</a> was performed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1914_population_statistics_for_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="1914 population statistics for the Ottoman Empire">1914</a>. Despite <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_military_reforms" title="Ottoman military reforms">military reforms</a> which reconstituted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Modern_Army" title="Ottoman Modern Army">Ottoman Modern Army</a>, the Empire lost its North African territories and the Dodecanese in the <a href="/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War" title="Italo-Turkish War">Italo-Turkish War</a> (1911) and almost all of its European territories in the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a> (1912â1913). The Empire faced continuous unrest in the years leading up to <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/31_March_Incident" title="31 March Incident">31 March Incident</a> and two further coups in <a href="/wiki/1912_Ottoman_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1912 Ottoman coup d'Ã©tat">1912</a> and <a href="/wiki/1913_Ottoman_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1913 Ottoman coup d'Ã©tat">1913</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_entry_into_World_War_I" title="Ottoman entry into World War I">Ottoman entry into World War I</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire_during_World_War_I" title="Ottoman Empire during World War I">Ottoman Empire during World War I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1222" data-file-width="1684" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg/220px-M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg/330px-M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg/440px-M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M_113_5_amiral_Souchon_et_ses_officiers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Admiral <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Souchon" title="Wilhelm Souchon">Wilhelm Souchon</a>, who commanded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Sea_Raid" title="Black Sea Raid">Black Sea Raid</a> on 29 October 1914, and his officers in Ottoman naval uniforms</div></div></div>
<p>The Ottoman Empire entered <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> and was ultimately defeated. The Ottoman participation in the war began with the combined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Sea_Raid" title="Black Sea Raid">German-Ottoman surprise attack</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coast of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> on 29 October 1914. Following the attack, the Russian Empire (2 November 1914)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxfordreference-timeline_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxfordreference-timeline-150">[142]</a></sup> and its allies <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a> (5 November 1914)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxfordreference-timeline_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxfordreference-timeline-150">[142]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> (5 November 1914)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxfordreference-timeline_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-oxfordreference-timeline-150">[142]</a></sup> declared war on the Ottoman Empire (also on 5 November 1914, the British government changed the status of the <a href="/wiki/Khedivate_of_Egypt" title="Khedivate of Egypt">Khedivate of Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_Convention" title="Cyprus Convention">Cyprus</a>, which were <a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a> Ottoman territories prior to the war, as <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorates</a>.)
</p><p>The Ottomans successfully defended the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> strait during the <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_campaign" title="Gallipoli campaign">Gallipoli campaign</a> (1915â1916) and achieved initial victories against British forces in the first two years of the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_campaign" title="Mesopotamian campaign">Mesopotamian campaign</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kut" title="Siege of Kut">Siege of Kut</a> (1915â1916); but the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a> (1916â1918) turned the tide against the Ottomans in the Middle East. In the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_campaign" title="Caucasus campaign">Caucasus campaign</a>, however, the Russian forces had the upper hand from the beginning, especially after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarikamish" title="Battle of Sarikamish">Battle of Sarikamish</a> (1914â1915). Russian forces advanced into northeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and controlled the major cities there until retreating from World War I with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a> in 1917.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Genocides">Genocides</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Late_Ottoman_genocides" title="Late Ottoman genocides">Late Ottoman genocides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seyfo" title="Seyfo">Seyfo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcharmenians.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1439" data-file-width="2193" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/220px-Marcharmenians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/330px-Marcharmenians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/440px-Marcharmenians.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcharmenians.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a> was the result of the Ottoman government's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tehcir_Law" title="Tehcir Law">deportation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> policies regarding its <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Armenians in the Ottoman Empire">Armenian</a> citizens after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarikamish" title="Battle of Sarikamish">Battle of Sarikamish</a> (1914â1915) and the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_campaign" title="Caucasus campaign">Caucasus Front</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">Imperial Russian Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_volunteer_units" title="Armenian volunteer units">Armenian volunteer units</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. An estimated 600,000 to more than 1 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup> or up to 1.5 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup> people were killed.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1915 the Ottoman government and Kurdish tribes in the region started the extermination of its ethnic Armenian population, resulting in the death of up to 1.5<span class="nowrap">Â </span>million Armenians in the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup> The genocide was carried out during and after World War I and implemented in two phases: the wholesale killing of the able-bodied male population through massacre and subjection of army conscripts to forced labour, followed by the deportation of women, children, the elderly and infirm on <a href="/wiki/Death_march" title="Death march">death marches</a> leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_desert" title="Syrian desert">Syrian desert</a>. Driven forward by military escorts, the deportees were deprived of food and water and subjected to periodic robbery, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_Armenian_Genocide" title="Rape during the Armenian Genocide">rape</a>, and systematic massacre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup> Large-scale massacres were also committed against the Empire's <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">Assyrian</a> minorities as part of the same campaign of ethnic cleansing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Revolt">Arab Revolt</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="Middle Eastern theatre of World War I">Middle Eastern theatre of World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a> began in 1916 with British support. It turned the tide against the Ottomans on the Middle Eastern front, where they seemed to have the upper hand during the first two years of the war. On the basis of the <a href="/wiki/McMahon%E2%80%93Hussein_Correspondence" title="McMahonâHussein Correspondence">McMahonâHussein Correspondence</a>, an agreement between the British government and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussein_bin_Ali,_Sharif_of_Mecca" title="Hussein bin Ali, Sharif of Mecca">Hussein bin Ali, Sharif of Mecca</a>, the revolt was officially initiated at Mecca on 10 June 1916.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[i]</a></sup> The Arab nationalist goal was to create a single unified and independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_state" title="Arab state">Arab state</a> stretching from <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, which the British had promised to recognise.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sharifian_Army" title="Sharifian Army">Sharifian Army</a> led by Hussein and the <a href="/wiki/Hashemites" title="Hashemites">Hashemites</a>, with military backing from the British <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Expeditionary_Force" title="Egyptian Expeditionary Force">Egyptian Expeditionary Force</a>, successfully fought and expelled the Ottoman military presence from much of the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_(region)" title="Transjordan (region)">Transjordan</a>. The rebellion eventually took <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and set up a short-lived monarchy led by <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">Faisal</a>, a son of Hussein.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Sykes%E2%80%93Picot_Agreement" title="SykesâPicot Agreement">SykesâPicot Agreement</a>, the Middle East was later partitioned by the British and French into <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">mandate territories</a>. There was no unified Arab state, much to the anger of Arab nationalists.
</p>
<h5><span id="Treaty_of_S.C3.A8vres_and_Turkish_War_of_Independence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_of_SÃ¨vres_and_Turkish_War_of_Independence">Treaty of SÃ¨vres and Turkish War of Independence</span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sultanvahideddin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="521" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Sultanvahideddin.jpg/220px-Sultanvahideddin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Sultanvahideddin.jpg/330px-Sultanvahideddin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Sultanvahideddin.jpg/440px-Sultanvahideddin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sultanvahideddin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_VI" title="Mehmed VI">Mehmed VI</a>, the last Sultan of the Ottoman Empire, leaving the country after the abolition of the Ottoman sultanate, 17 November 1922</div></div></div>
<p>Defeated on every front, the Ottoman Empire signed the <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_Mudros" title="Armistice of Mudros">Armistice of Mudros</a> on 30 October 1918. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople" title="Occupation of Constantinople">Constantinople was occupied</a> by combined British, French, Italian, and Greek forces. In May 1919, Greece also <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Smyrna" title="Occupation of Smyrna">took control of the area around Smyrna</a> (now Ä°zmir).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partition of the Ottoman Empire">partition of the Ottoman Empire</a> was finalized under the terms of the 1920 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of SÃ¨vres">Treaty of SÃ¨vres</a>. This treaty, as designed in the <a href="/wiki/Conference_of_London_(1920)" title="Conference of London (1920)">Conference of London</a>, allowed the Sultan to retain his position and title. The status of Anatolia was problematic given the occupied forces.
</p><p>There arose a nationalist opposition in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_national_movement" title="Turkish national movement">Turkish national movement</a>. It won the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_War_of_Independence" title="Turkish War of Independence">Turkish War of Independence</a> (1919â1923) under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal AtatÃ¼rk">Mustafa Kemal</a> (later given the surname "AtatÃ¼rk"). The sultanate was abolished on 1 November 1922, and the last sultan, <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_VI" title="Mehmed VI">Mehmed VI</a> (reigned 1918â1922), left the country on 17 November 1922. The <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> was <a href="/wiki/History_of_Turkey#Republic_of_Turkey" title="History of Turkey">established</a> in its place on 29 October 1923, in the new capital city of <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate" title="Ottoman Caliphate">caliphate</a> was abolished on 3 March 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ozoglu_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ozoglu-162">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiographical_debate_on_the_Ottoman_state">Historiographical debate on the Ottoman state</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ghaza_thesis" title="Ghaza thesis">Ghaza thesis</a></div>
<p>Several historians such as British historian <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> and the Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Dimitri_Kitsikis" title="Dimitri Kitsikis">Dimitri Kitsikis</a> have argued that after the fall of Constantinople, the Ottoman state took over the machinery of the Byzantine (Roman) state and that in essence, the Ottoman Empire was a continuation of the Eastern Roman Empire under a <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> guise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[154]</a></sup> The American historian <a href="/wiki/Speros_Vryonis" title="Speros Vryonis">Speros Vryonis</a> wrote that the Ottoman state was centered on "a Byzantine-Balkan base with a veneer of the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish language</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone,_pp._86-100_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stone,_pp._86-100-164">[155]</a></sup> The American historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heath_Lowry" title="Heath Lowry">Heath Lowry</a> and Kitsikis posit that the early Ottoman state was a predatory confederacy open to both Byzantine Christians and Turkish Muslims, whose primary goal was attaining booty and slaves, rather than spreading Islam, and that only later Islam became the primary characteristic of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[158]</a></sup> Other historians have followed the lead of the Austrian historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittek" title="Paul Wittek">Paul Wittek</a> who emphasized the Islamic character of the early Ottoman state, seeing the Ottoman state as a "<a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">Jihad</a> state" dedicated to expanding the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone,_pp._86-100_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stone,_pp._86-100-164">[155]</a></sup> Many historians led in 1937 by the Turkish historian <a href="/wiki/Mehmet_Fuat_K%C3%B6pr%C3%BCl%C3%BC" title="Mehmet Fuat KÃ¶prÃ¼lÃ¼">Mehmet Fuat KÃ¶prÃ¼lÃ¼</a> championed the <a href="/wiki/Ghaza_thesis" title="Ghaza thesis">Ghaza thesis</a> that saw the early Ottoman state as a continuation of the way of life of the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic tribes</a> who had come from East Asia to Anatolia via Central Asia and the Middle East on a much larger scale. They argued that the most important cultural influences on the Ottoman state came from <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>The British historian <a href="/wiki/Norman_Stone" title="Norman Stone">Norman Stone</a> suggested many continuities between the Eastern Roman and Ottoman empires such as the <i>zeugarion</i> tax of Byzantium becoming the Ottoman <i>Resm-i Ã§ift</i> tax, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pronoia" title="Pronoia">pronoia</a></i> land-holding system that linked the amount of land one owned with one's ability to raise cavalry becoming the Ottoman <i><a href="/wiki/Timar" title="Timar">timar</a></i> system, and the Ottoman measurement for land the <i>dÃ¶nÃ¼m</i> was the same as the Byzantine <i>stremma</i>. Stone also pointed out that despite the fact that Sunni Islam was the state religion, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> was supported and controlled by the Ottoman state, and in return to accepting that control became the largest land-holder in the Ottoman Empire. Despite the similarities, Stone argued that a crucial difference was that the land grants under the <i>timar</i> system were not hereditary at first. Even after land grants under the <i>timar</i> system became inheritable, land ownership in the Ottoman Empire remained highly insecure, and the sultan could and did revoke land grants whenever he wished. Stone argued this insecurity in land tenure strongly discouraged <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timariot" title="Timariot">Timariots</a></i> from seeking long-term development of their land, and instead led the <i>timariots</i> to adopt a strategy of short-term exploitation, which ultimately had deleterious effects on the Ottoman economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the Ottoman Sultans adhered to <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> and followed Sufi orders, and believed Sufism was the correct way to reach God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[161]</a></sup> Because the matters of jurisprudence and shariah were state matters, the state-sponsored Sufi religious dominance came into play. Non-Sufi Muslims and Arabs were neglected and not given any position in the Hejaz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_organisation_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="State organisation of the Ottoman Empire">State organisation of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2630" data-file-width="3554" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Baptiste_Vanmour_-_Dinner_at_the_Palace_in_Honour_of_an_Ambassador_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ambassadors at the <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="TopkapÄ± Palace">TopkapÄ± Palace</a></div></div></div>
<p>Before the reforms of the 19th and 20th centuries, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_organisation_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="State organisation of the Ottoman Empire">state organisation of the Ottoman Empire</a> was a system with two main dimensions, the military administration, and the civil administration. The Sultan was in the highest position in the system. The civil system was based on local administrative units based on the region's characteristics. The state had control over the clergy. Certain pre-Islamic Turkish traditions that had survived the adoption of administrative and legal practices from Islamic <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> remained important in Ottoman administrative circles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEItzkowitz198038_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEItzkowitz198038-172">[163]</a></sup> According to Ottoman understanding, the state's primary responsibility was to defend and extend the land of the Muslims and to ensure security and harmony within its borders in the overarching context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">orthodox</a> Islamic practice and dynastic sovereignty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kapucu_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kapucu-173">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ottoman Empire, or as a dynastic institution, the House of Osman, was unprecedented and unequaled in the Islamic world for its size and duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[165]</a></sup> In Europe, only the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">House of Habsburg</a> had a similarly unbroken line of sovereigns (kings/emperors) from the same family who ruled for so long, and during the same period, between the late 13th and early 20th centuries. The Ottoman dynasty was Turkish in origin. On eleven occasions, the sultan was deposed (replaced by another sultan of the Ottoman dynasty, who were either the former sultan's brother, son or nephew) because he was perceived by his enemies as a threat to the state. There were only two attempts in Ottoman history to unseat the ruling Ottoman dynasty, both failures, which suggests a political system that for an extended period was able to manage its revolutions without unnecessary instability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kapucu_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kapucu-173">[164]</a></sup> As such, the last Ottoman sultan Mehmed VI (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr>Â 1918â1922</span>) was a <a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">direct patrilineal (male-line) descendant</a> of the first Ottoman sultan <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman I</a> (<abbr title="died">d.</abbr> 1323/4), which was unparalleled in both Europe (e.g., the male line of the House of Habsburg became extinct in 1740) and in the Islamic world. The primary purpose of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Harem" title="Imperial Harem">Imperial Harem</a> was to ensure the birth of male heirs to the Ottoman throne and secure the continuation of the direct patrilineal (male-line) power of the Ottoman sultans in the future generations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5976" data-file-width="4980" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg/220px-Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg/330px-Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg/440px-Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_asv2020-02_img15_Topkap%C4%B1_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inside Harem, the private residence of the sultan in <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="TopkapÄ± Palace">TopkapÄ± Palace</a></div></div></div>
<p>The highest position in Islam, <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphate</a>, was claimed by the sultans starting with <a href="/wiki/Murad_I" title="Murad I">Murad I</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambton_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lambton-13">[12]</a></sup> which was established as the Ottoman Caliphate. The Ottoman sultan, <i><a href="/wiki/Padishah" title="Padishah">pÃ¢diÅÃ¢h</a></i> or "lord of kings", served as the Empire's sole regent and was considered to be the embodiment of its government, though he did not always exercise complete control. The Imperial Harem was one of the most important powers of the Ottoman court. It was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Valide_sultan" title="Valide sultan">valide sultan</a>. On occasion, the valide sultan would become involved in state politics. For a time, the women of the Harem effectively controlled the state in what was termed the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_the_women" title="Sultanate of the women">Sultanate of Women</a>". New sultans were always chosen from the sons of the previous sultan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="this is demonstrably not true, just look at any list of sultans (September 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> â <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ottoman_Empire#Dubious" title="Talk:Ottoman Empire">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> The strong educational system of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palace_school" title="Palace school">palace school</a> was geared towards eliminating the unfit potential heirs and establishing support among the ruling elite for a successor. The palace schools, which would also educate the future administrators of the state, were not a single track. First, the <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">Madrasa</a> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><i lang="ota">Medrese</i></span>) was designated for the Muslims, and educated scholars and state officials according to Islamic tradition. The financial burden of the Medrese was supported by <i><a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">vakif</a></i>s, allowing children of poor families to move to higher social levels and income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[166]</a></sup> The second track was a free <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarding school</a> for the Christians, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ender%C3%BBn" title="EnderÃ»n">EnderÃ»n</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[167]</a></sup> which recruited 3,000 students annually from Christian boys between eight and twenty years old from one in forty families among the communities settled in <a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a> or the Balkans, a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devshirme_in_the_Ottoman_Palace_School" title="Devshirme in the Ottoman Palace School">Devshirme</a> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><i lang="ota">DevÅirme</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though the sultan was the supreme monarch, the sultan's political and executive authority was delegated. The politics of the state had a number of advisors and ministers gathered around a council known as <a href="/wiki/Divan" title="Divan">Divan</a>. The Divan, in the years when the Ottoman state was still a <i><a href="/wiki/Bey" title="Bey">Beylik</a></i>, was composed of the elders of the tribe. Its composition was later modified to include military officers and local elites (such as religious and political advisors). Later still, beginning in 1320, a Grand Vizier was appointed to assume certain of the sultan's responsibilities. The Grand Vizier had considerable independence from the sultan with almost unlimited powers of appointment, dismissal, and supervision. Beginning with the late 16th century, sultans withdrew from politics and the Grand Vizier became the <i>de facto</i> head of state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Black_p199_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Black_p199-178">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="879" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg/220px-Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg/330px-Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg/440px-Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yusuf_Ziya_Pa%C5%9Fa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yusuf_Ziya_Pasha" title="Yusuf Ziya Pasha">Yusuf Ziya Pasha</a>, Ottoman ambassador to the United States, in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a>, 1913</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout Ottoman history, there were many instances in which local governors acted independently, and even in opposition to the ruler. After the Young Turk Revolution of 1908, the Ottoman state became a constitutional monarchy. The sultan no longer had executive powers. A parliament was formed, with representatives chosen from the provinces. The representatives formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Imperial Government of the Ottoman Empire">Imperial Government of the Ottoman Empire</a>.
</p><p>This eclectic administration was apparent even in the diplomatic correspondence of the Empire, which was initially undertaken in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> to the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tughra" title="Tughra">Tughra</a> were calligraphic monograms, or signatures, of the Ottoman Sultans, of which there were 35. Carved on the Sultan's seal, they bore the names of the Sultan and his father. The statement and prayer, "ever victorious", was also present in most. The earliest belonged to Orhan Gazi. The ornately stylized <i>Tughra</i> spawned a branch of Ottoman-Turkish <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_law" title="Ottoman law">Ottoman law</a></div>
<p>The Ottoman legal system accepted the <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious law</a> over its subjects. At the same time the <i><a href="/wiki/Qanun_(law)" title="Qanun (law)">Qanun</a></i> (or <i>Kanun</i>), dynastic law, co-existed with religious law or <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-otmkanun_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-otmkanun-180">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[172]</a></sup> The Ottoman Empire was always organized around a system of local <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>. Legal administration in the Ottoman Empire was part of a larger scheme of balancing central and local authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton_109-110_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benton_109-110-182">[173]</a></sup> Ottoman power revolved crucially around the administration of the rights to land, which gave a space for the local authority to develop the needs of the local <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton_109-110_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benton_109-110-182">[173]</a></sup> The jurisdictional complexity of the Ottoman Empire was aimed to permit the integration of culturally and religiously different groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton_109-110_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-Benton_109-110-182">[173]</a></sup> The Ottoman system had three court systems: one for Muslims, one for non-Muslims, involving appointed Jews and Christians ruling over their respective religious communities, and the "trade court". The entire system was regulated from above by means of the administrative <i>Qanun</i>, i.e., laws, a system based upon the Turkic <i><a href="/wiki/Yassa" title="Yassa">Yassa</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=T%C3%B6re_(law)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TÃ¶re (law) (page does not exist)">TÃ¶re</a></i>, which were developed in the pre-Islamic era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/290px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/435px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/580px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Ottoman trial, 1877</div></div></div>
<p>These court categories were not, however, wholly exclusive; for instance, the Islamic courts, which were the Empire's primary courts, could also be used to settle a trade conflict or disputes between litigants of differing religions, and Jews and Christians often went to them to obtain a more forceful ruling on an issue. The Ottoman state tended not to interfere with non-Muslim religious law systems, despite legally having a voice to do so through local governors. The Islamic <i>Sharia</i> law system had been developed from a combination of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">HadÄ«th</a>, or words of the prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijmÄ'</a></i>, or consensus of the members of the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a>; <a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a>, a system of analogical reasoning from earlier precedents; and local customs. Both systems were taught at the Empire's law schools, which were in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bursa" title="Bursa">Bursa</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zibik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2810" data-file-width="1476" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Zibik.jpg/170px-Zibik.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Zibik.jpg/255px-Zibik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Zibik.jpg/340px-Zibik.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zibik.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unhappy wife complains to the <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">Qadi</a> about her husband's impotence as depicted in an <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniature</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Ottoman Islamic legal system was set up differently from traditional European courts. Presiding over Islamic courts would be a <i>Qadi</i>, or judge. Since the closing of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a></i>, or 'Gate of Interpretation', <i>Qadi</i>s throughout the Ottoman Empire focused less on legal precedent, and more with local customs and traditions in the areas that they administered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton_109-110_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-Benton_109-110-182">[173]</a></sup> However, the Ottoman court system lacked an appellate structure, leading to jurisdictional case strategies where plaintiffs could take their disputes from one court system to another until they achieved a ruling that was in their favour.
</p><p>In the late 19th century, the Ottoman legal system saw substantial reform. This process of legal modernisation began with the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_G%C3%BClhane" title="Edict of GÃ¼lhane">Edict of GÃ¼lhane</a> of 1839.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review-niza_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-review-niza-185">[176]</a></sup> These reforms included the "fair and public trial[s] of all accused regardless of religion", the creation of a system of "separate competences, religious and civil", and the validation of testimony on non-Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int-handbook_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-int-handbook-186">[177]</a></sup> Specific land codes (1858), civil codes (1869â1876), and a code of civil procedure also were enacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int-handbook_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-int-handbook-186">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>These reforms were based heavily on French models, as indicated by the adoption of a three-tiered court system. Referred to as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nizamiye&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nizamiye (page does not exist)">Nizamiye</a>, this system was extended to the local magistrate level with the final promulgation of the <a href="/wiki/Mecelle" title="Mecelle">Mecelle</a>, a civil code that regulated marriage, divorce, alimony, will, and other matters of personal status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int-handbook_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-int-handbook-186">[177]</a></sup> In an attempt to clarify the division of judicial competences, an administrative council laid down that religious matters were to be handled by religious courts, and statute matters were to be handled by the Nizamiye courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int-handbook_186-3"><a href="#cite_note-int-handbook-186">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Military of the Ottoman Empire">Military of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg/220px-Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg/330px-Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg/440px-Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walka_o_sztandar_turecki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Sipahi" title="Sipahi">sipahis</a> in battle, holding the crescent banner (by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Brandt" title="JÃ³zef Brandt">JÃ³zef Brandt</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The first military unit of the Ottoman State was an army that was organized by Osman I from the tribesmen inhabiting the hills of western Anatolia in the late 13th century. The military system became an intricate organization with the advance of the Empire. The Ottoman military was a complex system of recruiting and fief-holding. The main corps of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Army" title="Ottoman Army">Ottoman Army</a> included Janissary, <a href="/wiki/Sipahi" title="Sipahi">Sipahi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akinji" title="Akinji">AkÄ±ncÄ±</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_military_band" title="Ottoman military band">MehterÃ¢n</a>. The Ottoman army was once among the most advanced fighting forces in the world, being one of the first to use muskets and cannons. The Ottoman Turks began using <i><a href="/wiki/Falconet_(cannon)" title="Falconet (cannon)">falconets</a></i>, which were short but wide cannons, during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1422)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1422)">Siege of Constantinople</a>. The Ottoman cavalry depended on high speed and mobility rather than heavy armor, using bows and short swords on fast <a href="/wiki/Turkoman_horse" title="Turkoman horse">Turkoman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian</a> horses (progenitors of the <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred#Foundation_stallions" title="Thoroughbred">Thoroughbred</a> racing horse),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[179]</a></sup> and often applied tactics similar to those of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, such as pretending to retreat while surrounding the enemy forces inside a crescent-shaped formation and then making the real attack. The Ottoman army continued to be an effective fighting force throughout the seventeenth and early eighteenth centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[181]</a></sup> falling behind the empire's European rivals only during a long period of peace from 1740 to 1768.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AksanOW_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-AksanOW-33">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
