<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Culture of the Ottoman Empire">Culture of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WaldmeierLebanon.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="706" data-file-width="948" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/220px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/330px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/440px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WaldmeierLebanon.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Hookah" title="Hookah">hookah</a> shop in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, Ottoman Empire</div></div></div>
<p>The Ottomans absorbed some of the traditions, art, and institutions of cultures in the regions they conquered and added new dimensions to them. Numerous traditions and cultural traits of previous empires (In fields such as architecture, cuisine, music, leisure, and government) were adopted by the Ottoman Turks, who developed them into new forms, resulting in a new and distinctively Ottoman cultural identity. Although the predominant literary language of the Ottoman Empire was Turkish, Persian was the preferred vehicle for the projection of an imperial image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Slavery (Ottoman Empire)">Slavery</a> was a part of Ottoman society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[244]</a></sup> with most slaves employed as domestic servants. Agricultural slavery, such as that which was widespread in the Americas, was relatively rare. Unlike systems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chattel_slavery" title="Chattel slavery">chattel slavery</a>, slaves under Islamic law were not regarded as movable property, and the children of female slaves were born legally free. Female slaves were still sold in the Empire as late as 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[245]</a></sup> During the 19th century the Empire came under pressure from Western European countries to outlaw the practice. Policies developed by various sultans throughout the 19th century attempted to curtail the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_slave_trade" title="Ottoman slave trade">Ottoman slave trade</a> but slavery had centuries of religious backing and sanction and so slavery was never abolished in the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-237">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">Plague</a> remained a major scourge in Ottoman society until the second quarter of the 19th century. "Between 1701 and 1750, 37 larger and smaller plague epidemics were recorded in Istanbul, and 31 between 1751 and 1801."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ottomans adopted Persian bureaucratic traditions and culture. The sultans also made an important contribution in the development of Persian literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[247]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Education in the Ottoman Empire">Education in the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_(14667026514).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1226" data-file-width="1696" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/220px-Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/330px-Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/440px-Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_(14667026514).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>BeyazÄ±t State Library was founded in 1884.</div></div></div>
<p>In the Ottoman Empire, each <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a> established a schooling system serving its members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StraussPT194195_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-StraussPT194195-257">[248]</a></sup> Education, therefore, was largely divided on ethnic and religious lines: few non-Muslims attended schools for Muslim students and vice versa. Most institutions that did serve all ethnic and religious groups taught in French or other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StraussPT195_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-StraussPT195-258">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_literature" title="Ottoman literature">Ottoman literature</a></div>
<p>The two primary streams of Ottoman written literature are poetry and <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a>. Poetry was by far the dominant stream. Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose did not contain any examples of fiction: there were no counterparts to, for instance, the European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">romance</a>, short story, or novel. Analog genres did exist, though, in both <a href="/wiki/Turkish_folk_literature" title="Turkish folk literature">Turkish folk literature</a> and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divan_poetry" title="Divan poetry">Divan poetry</a>.
</p><p>Ottoman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divan_poetry" title="Divan poetry">Divan poetry</a> was a highly ritualized and symbolic art form. From the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_poetry" title="Persian poetry">Persian poetry</a> that largely inspired it, it inherited a wealth of symbols whose meanings and interrelationshipsâboth of similitude (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota">ÙØ±Ø§Ø¹Ø§Øª ÙØ¸ÙØ±</span></span> <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">mura'Ã¢t-i nazÃ®r</i></span> / <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota">ØªÙØ§Ø³Ø¨</span></span> <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">tenÃ¢sÃ¼b</i></span>) and opposition (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota">ØªØ¶Ø§Ø¯</span></span> <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">tezÃ¢d</i></span>) were more or less prescribed. Divan poetry was composed through the constant juxtaposition of many such images within a strict metrical framework, thus allowing numerous potential meanings to emerge. The vast majority of Divan poetry was <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric</a> in nature: either <a href="/wiki/Gazel" title="Gazel">gazels</a> (which make up the greatest part of the repertoire of the tradition), or kasÃ®des. There were, however, other common genres, most particularly the mesnevÃ®, a kind of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courtly_romance" title="Courtly romance">verse romance</a> and thus a variety of <a href="/wiki/Narrative_poetry" title="Narrative poetry">narrative poetry</a>; the two most notable examples of this form are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leyli_and_Majnun" title="Leyli and Majnun">Leyli and Majnun</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuz%C3%BBl%C3%AE" title="FuzÃ»lÃ®">FuzÃ»lÃ®</a> and the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCsn_%C3%BC_A%C5%9Fk" title="HÃ¼sn Ã¼ AÅk">HÃ¼sn Ã¼ AÅk</a> of <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eeyh_G%C3%A2lib" title="Åeyh GÃ¢lib">Åeyh GÃ¢lib</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Seyahatn%C3%A2me" title="SeyahatnÃ¢me">SeyahatnÃ¢me</a> of <a href="/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi" title="Evliya Ãelebi">Evliya Ãelebi</a> (1611â1682) is an outstanding example of travel literature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nedim_(divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="631" data-file-width="490" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/170px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/255px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/340px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nedim_(divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmet_Ned%C3%AEm_Efendi" title="Ahmet NedÃ®m Efendi">Ahmet NedÃ®m Efendi</a>, one of the most celebrated Ottoman poets</div></div></div>
<p>Until the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Prose_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Prose of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman prose</a> did not develop to the extent that contemporary Divan poetry did. A large part of the reason for this was that much prose was expected to adhere to the rules of <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">sec</i></span> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota">Ø³Ø¬Ø¹</span></span>, also transliterated as <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">seci</i></span>), or <a href="/wiki/Rhymed_prose" title="Rhymed prose">rhymed prose</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[250]</a></sup> a type of writing descended from the Arabic <a href="/wiki/Saj%27" title="Saj'">saj'</a> and which prescribed that between each adjective and <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">noun</a> in a string of words, such as a sentence, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">rhyme</a>. Nevertheless, there was a tradition of prose in the literature of the time, though exclusively non-fictional in nature. One apparent exception was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhayyel%C3%A2t" title="MuhayyelÃ¢t">MuhayyelÃ¢t</a> ("Fancies") by <a href="/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi" title="Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi">Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi</a>, a collection of stories of the fantastic written in 1796, though not published until 1867. The first novel published in the Ottoman Empire was by an Armenian named <a href="/wiki/Vartan_Pasha" title="Vartan Pasha">Vartan Pasha</a>. Published in 1851, the novel was entitled The Story of Akabi (Turkish: Akabi Hikyayesi) and was written in Turkish but with <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to historically close ties with France, <a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a> came to constitute the major Western influence on Ottoman literature throughout the latter half of the 19th century. As a result, many of the same movements prevalent in France during this period also had their Ottoman equivalents; in the developing Ottoman prose tradition, for instance, the influence of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> can be seen during the Tanzimat period, and that of the <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">Realist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">Naturalist</a> movements in subsequent periods; in the poetic tradition, on the other hand, it was the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Symbolism_(arts)" title="Symbolism (arts)">Symbolist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parnassian_poets" title="Parnassian poets">Parnassian</a> movements that became paramount.
</p><p>Many of the writers in the Tanzimat period wrote in several different genres simultaneously; for instance, the poet <a href="/wiki/Nam%C4%B1k_Kemal" title="NamÄ±k Kemal">NamÄ±k Kemal</a> also wrote the important 1876 novel Ä°ntibÃ¢h ("Awakening"), while the journalist <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0brahim_%C5%9Einasi" title="Ä°brahim Åinasi">Ä°brahim Åinasi</a> is noted for writing, in 1860, the first modern Turkish play, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_act_play" title="One act play">one-act</a> comedy "<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">Åair Evlenmesi</i></span>" ("The Poet's Marriage"). An earlier play, a <a href="/wiki/Farce" title="Farce">farce</a> entitled "<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">VakÃ¢yi'-i 'Acibe ve HavÃ¢dis-i Garibe-yi KefÅger Ahmed</i></span>" ("The Strange Events and Bizarre Occurrences of the Cobbler Ahmed"), dates from the beginning of the 19th century, but there remains some doubt about its authenticity. In a similar vein, the novelist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmed_Midhat_Efendi" title="Ahmed Midhat Efendi">Ahmed Midhat Efendi</a> wrote important novels in each of the major movements: Romanticism (Hasan MellÃ¢h yÃ¢hud SÄ±rr Ä°Ã§inde EsrÃ¢r, 1873; "Hasan the Sailor, or The Mystery Within the Mystery"), Realism (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">HenÃ¼z on Yedi YaÅÄ±nda</i></span>, 1881; "Just Seventeen Years Old"), and Naturalism (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn">MÃ¼ÅÃ¢hedÃ¢t</i></span>, 1891; "Observations"). This diversity was, in part, due to the Tanzimat writers' wish to disseminate as much of the new literature as possible, in the hopes that it would contribute to a revitalization of Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ottoman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2019</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Media of the Ottoman Empire">Media of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2840" data-file-width="3301" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/220px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/330px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/440px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque" title="SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque">SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque</a> in Istanbul, designed by <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Sinan</a> in the 16th century and a major example of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture" title="Classical Ottoman architecture">Classical Ottoman style</a></div></div></div>
<p>The architecture of the empire developed from earlier <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_architecture" title="Seljuk architecture">Seljuk Turkish architecture</a>, with influences from <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_architecture" title="Iranian architecture">Iranian</a> architecture and other architectural traditions in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-:24-266">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-267">[258]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Early_Ottoman_architecture" title="Early Ottoman architecture">Early Ottoman architecture</a> experimented with multiple building types over the course of the 13th to 15th centuries, progressively evolving into the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture" title="Classical Ottoman architecture">Classical Ottoman style</a> of the 16th and 17th centuries, which was also strongly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-267">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodwin_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin-268">[259]</a></sup> The most important architect of the Classical period is <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Mimar Sinan</a>, whose major works include the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eehzade_Mosque" title="Åehzade Mosque">Åehzade Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque" title="SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque">SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Selimiye_Mosque,_Edirne" title="Selimiye Mosque, Edirne">Selimiye Mosque</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgostonMasters20102_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-AgostonMasters20102-269">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove_encycl-Ottoman_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grove_encycl-Ottoman-270">[261]</a></sup> The greatest of the court artists enriched the Ottoman Empire with many pluralistic artistic influences, such as mixing traditional <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a> with elements of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_art" title="Chinese art">Chinese art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[262]</a></sup> The second half of the 16th century also saw the apogee of certain decorative arts, most notably in the use of <a href="/wiki/Iznik_pottery" title="Iznik pottery">Iznik tiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the 18th century, Ottoman architecture was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a> in Western Europe, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Baroque_architecture" title="Ottoman Baroque architecture">Ottoman Baroque</a> style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-273">[264]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nuruosmaniye_Mosque" title="Nuruosmaniye Mosque">Nuruosmaniye Mosque</a> is one of the most important examples from this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355-274">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526-275">[266]</a></sup> The last Ottoman period saw more influences from Western Europe, brought in by architects such as those from the <a href="/wiki/Balyan_family" title="Balyan family">Balyan family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-276">[267]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire style</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> motifs were introduced and a trend towards <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism_in_architecture" title="Eclecticism in architecture">eclecticism</a> was evident in many types of buildings, such as the <a href="/wiki/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace" title="DolmabahÃ§e Palace">DolmabaÃ§e Palace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605â606_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605â606-277">[268]</a></sup> The last decades of the Ottoman Empire also saw the development of a new architectural style called neo-Ottoman or Ottoman revivalism, also known as the <a href="/wiki/First_national_architectural_movement" title="First national architectural movement">First National Architectural Movement</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BloomBloom20092_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-BloomBloom20092-278">[269]</a></sup> by architects such as <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Kemaleddin" title="Mimar Kemaleddin">Mimar Kemaleddin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vedat_Tek" title="Vedat Tek">Vedat Tek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-276">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ottoman dynastic patronage was concentrated in the historic capitals of Bursa, Edirne, and Istanbul (Constantinople), as well as in several other important administrative centers such as <a href="/wiki/Amasya" title="Amasya">Amasya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manisa" title="Manisa">Manisa</a>. It was in these centers that most important developments in Ottoman architecture occurred and that the most monumental Ottoman architecture can be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679-279">[270]</a></sup> Major religious monuments were typically architectural complexes, known as a <i><a href="/wiki/K%C3%BClliye" title="KÃ¼lliye">kÃ¼lliye</a></i>, that had multiple components providing different services or amenities. In addition to a <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>, these could include a <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hammam" title="Hammam">hammam</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Imaret" title="Imaret">imaret</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sebil_(fountain)" title="Sebil (fountain)">sebil</a>, a market, a <a href="/wiki/Caravanserai" title="Caravanserai">caravanserai</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Kuttab" title="Kuttab">primary school</a>, or others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-280">[271]</a></sup> These complexes were governed and managed with the help of a <i>vakif</i> agreement (Arabic <i>waqf</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-280">[271]</a></sup> Ottoman constructions were still abundant in Anatolia and in the Balkans (Rumelia), but in the more distant Middle Eastern and North African provinces older <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architectural</a> styles continued to hold strong influence and were sometimes blended with Ottoman styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571â596_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571â596-281">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251-282">[273]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_arts">Decorative arts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3151" data-file-width="1925" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/170px-Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/255px-Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/340px-Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniature</a> lost its function with the Westernization of Ottoman culture.</div></div></div>
<p>The tradition of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniatures</a>, painted to illustrate manuscripts or used in dedicated albums, was heavily influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian</a> art form, though it also included elements of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine</a> tradition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuscript_illumination" title="Manuscript illumination">illumination</a> and painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[274]</a></sup> A Greek academy of painters, the <i>Nakkashane-i-Rum</i>, was established in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topkapi_Palace" title="Topkapi Palace">Topkapi Palace</a> in the 15th century, while early in the following century a similar Persian academy, the <i>Nakkashane-i-Irani</i>, was added. <a href="/wiki/Surname-i_H%C3%BCmayun" title="Surname-i HÃ¼mayun">Surname-i HÃ¼mayun</a> (Imperial Festival Books) were albums that commemorated celebrations in the Ottoman Empire in pictorial and textual detail.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_illumination" title="Ottoman illumination">Ottoman illumination</a> covers non-figurative painted or drawn decorative art in books or on sheets in <i><a href="/wiki/Muraqqa" title="Muraqqa">muraqqa</a></i> or albums, as opposed to the figurative images of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniature</a>. It was a part of the Ottoman Book Arts together with the Ottoman miniature (<i>taswir</i>), calligraphy (<i>hat</i>), <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a>, bookbinding (<i>cilt</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Paper_marbling" title="Paper marbling">paper marbling</a> (<i>ebru</i>). In the Ottoman Empire, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated and illustrated manuscripts</a> were commissioned by the Sultan or the administrators of the court. In Topkapi Palace, these manuscripts were created by the artists working in <i>Nakkashane</i>, the atelier of the miniature and illumination artists. Both religious and non-religious books could be illuminated. Also, sheets for albums <i>levha</i> consisted of illuminated calligraphy (<i>hat</i>) of <i><a href="/wiki/Tughra" title="Tughra">tughra</a></i>, religious texts, verses from poems or proverbs, and purely decorative drawings.
</p><p>The art of carpet <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> was particularly significant in the Ottoman Empire, carpets having an immense importance both as decorative furnishings, rich in religious and other symbolism and as a practical consideration, as it was customary to remove one's shoes in living quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foroqhi_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-foroqhi-284">[275]</a></sup> The weaving of such carpets originated in the <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> cultures of central Asia (carpets being an easily transportable form of furnishing), and eventually spread to the settled societies of Anatolia. Turks used carpets, rugs, and <a href="/wiki/Kilim" title="Kilim">kilims</a> not just on the floors of a room but also as a hanging on walls and doorways, where they provided additional insulation. They were also commonly donated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosques" title="Mosques">mosques</a>, which often amassed large collections of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foroqhip153_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-foroqhip153-285">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_performing_arts">Music and performing arts</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_classical_music" title="Ottoman classical music">Ottoman classical music</a> was an important part of the education of the Ottoman elite. A number of the Ottoman sultans have accomplished musicians and composers themselves, such as <a href="/wiki/Selim_III" title="Selim III">Selim III</a>, whose compositions are often still performed today. Ottoman classical music arose largely from a confluence of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Byzantine music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_music" title="Armenian music">Armenian music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_traditional_music" title="Persian traditional music">Persian music</a>. Compositionally, it is organized around rhythmic units called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Usul_(music)" title="Usul (music)">usul</a>, which are somewhat similar to <a href="/wiki/Metre_(music)" title="Metre (music)">meter</a> in Western music, and <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodic</a> units called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makam" title="Makam">makam</a>, which bear some resemblance to Western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_mode" title="Musical mode">musical modes</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">instruments</a> used are a mixture of Anatolian and Central Asian instruments (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baglama" title="Baglama">saz</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baglama" title="Baglama">baÄlama</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kemenche" title="Kemenche">kemence</a>), other Middle Eastern instruments (the <a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">ud</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tanbur" title="Tanbur">tanbur</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Qanun_(instrument)" title="Qanun (instrument)">kanun</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ney" title="Ney">ney</a>), andâlater in the traditionâWestern instruments (the violin and the piano). Because of a geographic and cultural divide between the capital and other areas, two broadly distinct styles of music arose in the Ottoman Empire: Ottoman classical music and folk music. In the provinces, several different kinds of <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a> were created. The most dominant regions with their distinguished musical styles are Balkan-Thracian TÃ¼rkÃ¼s, North-Eastern (<a href="/wiki/Laz_people" title="Laz people">Laz</a>) TÃ¼rkÃ¼s, Aegean TÃ¼rkÃ¼s, Central Anatolian TÃ¼rkÃ¼s, Eastern Anatolian TÃ¼rkÃ¼s, and Caucasian TÃ¼rkÃ¼s. Some of the distinctive styles were: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_military_band" title="Ottoman military band">Janissary Music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roma_music" title="Roma music">Roma music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belly_dance" title="Belly dance">Belly dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_folk_music" title="Turkish folk music">Turkish folk music</a>.
</p><p>The traditional <a href="/wiki/Shadow_play" title="Shadow play">shadow play</a> called <a href="/wiki/Karag%C3%B6z_and_Hacivat" title="KaragÃ¶z and Hacivat">KaragÃ¶z and Hacivat</a> was widespread throughout the Ottoman Empire and featured characters representing all of the major ethnic and social groups in that culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[278]</a></sup> It was performed by a single puppet master, who voiced all of the characters, and accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Tambourine" title="Tambourine">tambourine</a> (<i>def</i>). Its origins are obscure, deriving perhaps from an older Egyptian tradition, or possibly from an Asian source.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine" title="Ottoman cuisine">Ottoman cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin,_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3092" data-file-width="2247" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/170px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/255px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/340px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin,_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turkish women baking bread, 1790</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine" title="Ottoman cuisine">Ottoman cuisine</a> refers to the cuisine of the capital, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a> (<a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>), and the regional capital cities, where the melting pot of cultures created a common cuisine that most of the population regardless of ethnicity shared. This diverse cuisine was honed in the Imperial Palace's kitchens by chefs brought from certain parts of the Empire to create and experiment with different ingredients. The creations of the Ottoman Palace's kitchens filtered to the population, for instance through <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> events, and through the cooking at the <a href="/wiki/Yal%C4%B1" title="YalÄ±">YalÄ±s</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pashas</a>, and from there on spread to the rest of the population.
</p><p>Much of the cuisine of former Ottoman territories today is descended from a shared Ottoman cuisine, especially <a href="/wiki/Turkish_cuisine" title="Turkish cuisine">Turkish</a>, and including <a href="/wiki/Greek_cuisine" title="Greek cuisine">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balkan_cuisine" title="Balkan cuisine">Balkan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_cuisine" title="Armenian cuisine">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_cuisine" title="Middle Eastern cuisine">Middle Eastern</a> cuisines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fragner_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fragner-288">[279]</a></sup> Many common dishes in the region, descendants of the once-common Ottoman cuisine, include <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%C3%B6ner_kebab" title="DÃ¶ner kebab">dÃ¶ner kebab</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyro_(food)" title="Gyro (food)">gyro</a>/<a href="/wiki/Shawarma" title="Shawarma">shawarma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cac%C4%B1k" title="CacÄ±k">cacÄ±k</a>/tzatziki, <a href="/wiki/Ayran" title="Ayran">ayran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pita" title="Pita">pita</a> bread, <a href="/wiki/Feta" title="Feta">feta</a> cheese, <a href="/wiki/Baklava" title="Baklava">baklava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahmacun" title="Lahmacun">lahmacun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moussaka" title="Moussaka">moussaka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuvarlak" title="Yuvarlak">yuvarlak</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%B6fte" title="KÃ¶fte">kÃ¶fte</a>/keftÃ©s/kofta, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6rek" title="BÃ¶rek">bÃ¶rek</a>/boureki, <a href="/wiki/Rak%C4%B1" title="RakÄ±">rakÄ±</a>/<a href="/wiki/Rakia" title="Rakia">rakia</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tsipouro" title="Tsipouro">tsipouro</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tsikoudia" title="Tsikoudia">tsikoudia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meze" title="Meze">meze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolma" title="Dolma">dolma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarma_(food)" title="Sarma (food)">sarma</a>, rice <a href="/wiki/Pilaf" title="Pilaf">pilaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_coffee" title="Turkish coffee">Turkish coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sujuk" title="Sujuk">sujuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashk" title="Kashk">kashk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ke%C5%9Fkek" title="KeÅkek">keÅkek</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manti_(dumpling)" title="Manti (dumpling)">manti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lavash" title="Lavash">lavash</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanafeh" title="Kanafeh">kanafeh</a>, and more.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire">Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2029" data-file-width="2356" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/220px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/330px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/440px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantinople_Observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din" title="Constantinople Observatory of Taqi ad-Din">Observatory of Taqi ad-Din</a> in 1577</div></div></div>
<p>Over the course of Ottoman history, the Ottomans managed to build a large collection of libraries complete with translations of books from other cultures, as well as original manuscripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ãgoston_and_Alan_Masters583_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ãgoston_and_Alan_Masters583-64">[56]</a></sup> A great part of this desire for local and foreign manuscripts arose in the 15th century. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmet_II" title="Mehmet II">Sultan Mehmet II</a> ordered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgios_Amiroutzes" title="Georgios Amiroutzes">Georgios Amiroutzes</a>, a Greek scholar from <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trabzon</a>, to translate and make available to Ottoman educational institutions the geography book of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>. Another example is <a href="/wiki/Ali_Qushji" title="Ali Qushji">Ali Qushji</a> â an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_mathematics" title="Islamic mathematics">mathematician</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_physics" title="Islamic physics">physicist</a> originally from <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> â who became a professor in two madrasas and influenced Ottoman circles as a result of his writings and the activities of his students, even though he only spent two or three years in Constantinople before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[280]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taqi_al-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf" title="Taqi al-Din Muhammad ibn Ma'ruf">Taqi al-Din</a> built the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople_observatory_of_Taqi_al-Din&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Constantinople observatory of Taqi al-Din (page does not exist)">Constantinople observatory of Taqi al-Din</a> in 1577, where he carried out observations until 1580. He calculated the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of the Sun's orbit and the annual motion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tekeli_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tekeli-290">[281]</a></sup> However, the observatory's primary purpose was almost certainly <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> rather than astronomical, leading to its destruction in 1580 due to the rise of a clerical faction that opposed its use for that purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[282]</a></sup> He also experimented with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_power" title="Steam power">steam power</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Egypt" title="Ottoman Egypt">Ottoman Egypt</a> in 1551, when he described a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_jack" title="Steam jack">steam jack</a> driven by a rudimentary <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[283]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="695" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg/220px-Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg/330px-Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg/440px-Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Osman-hamdi-bey-girl-reciting-qu-ran-1880.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Girl Reciting the QurÄn</i> (<i>Kuran Okuyan KÄ±z</i>), an 1880 painting by the Ottoman polymath <a href="/wiki/Osman_Hamdi_Bey" title="Osman Hamdi Bey">Osman Hamdi Bey</a>, whose works often showed women engaged in educational activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[284]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1660 the Ottoman scholar <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ibrahim_Efendi_al-Zigetvari_Tezkireci&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ibrahim Efendi al-Zigetvari Tezkireci (page does not exist)">Ibrahim Efendi al-Zigetvari Tezkireci</a> translated <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Duret" title="NoÃ«l Duret">NoÃ«l Duret</a>'s French astronomical work (written in 1637) into Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9Eerafeddin_Sabuncuo%C4%9Flu" title="Åerafeddin SabuncuoÄlu">Åerafeddin SabuncuoÄlu</a> was the author of the first surgical atlas and the last major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicine_in_medieval_Islam" title="Medicine in medieval Islam">medical encyclopaedia from the Islamic world</a>. Though his work was largely based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_al-Qasim_al-Zahrawi" title="Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi">Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Tasrif" title="Al-Tasrif">Al-Tasrif</a></i>, SabuncuoÄlu introduced many innovations of his own. Female surgeons were also illustrated for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[286]</a></sup> Since, the Ottoman Empire is credited with the invention of several surgical instruments in use such as <a href="/wiki/Forceps" title="Forceps">forceps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catheter" title="Catheter">catheters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalpels" title="Scalpels">scalpels and lancets</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Pincers_(tool)" title="Pincers (tool)">pincers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[287]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (April 2022)">betterÂ sourceÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>An example of a watch that measured time in minutes was created by an Ottoman watchmaker, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Meshur_Sheyh_Dede&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Meshur Sheyh Dede (page does not exist)">Meshur Sheyh Dede</a>, in 1702.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 19th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egypt_under_Muhammad_Ali" title="Egypt under Muhammad Ali">Egypt under Muhammad Ali</a> began using <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> for industrial manufacturing, with industries such as <a href="/wiki/Ironworks" title="Ironworks">ironworks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_mill" title="Paper mill">paper mills</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulling" title="Hulling">hulling</a> mills moving towards steam power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-batou193_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-batou193-298">[289]</a></sup> Economic historian Jean Batou argues that the necessary economic conditions existed in Egypt for the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> as a potential energy source for its steam engines later in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-batou193_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-batou193-298">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Ishak_Efendi" title="Ishak Efendi">Ishak Efendi</a> is credited with introducing the then current Western scientific ideas and developments to the Ottoman and wider Muslim world, as well as the invention of a suitable Turkish and Arabic scientific terminology, through his translations of Western works.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MedyaBJK.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="460" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/MedyaBJK.jpg/220px-MedyaBJK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/MedyaBJK.jpg/330px-MedyaBJK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/MedyaBJK.jpg/440px-MedyaBJK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MedyaBJK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of <a href="/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K." title="BeÅiktaÅ J.K.">BeÅiktaÅ J.K.</a> in 1903</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GS_Kuruculari.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="897" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/GS_Kuruculari.jpg/220px-GS_Kuruculari.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/GS_Kuruculari.jpg/330px-GS_Kuruculari.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/GS_Kuruculari.jpg/440px-GS_Kuruculari.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GS_Kuruculari.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of <a href="/wiki/Galatasaray_S.K._(football)" title="Galatasaray S.K. (football)">Galatasaray S.K. (football)</a> in 1905</div></div></div>
<p>The main sports Ottomans were engaged in were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_wrestling" title="Turkish wrestling">Turkish wrestling</a>, hunting, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_archery" title="Turkish archery">Turkish archery</a>, horseback riding, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirit" title="Cirit">equestrian javelin throw</a>, arm wrestling, and swimming. European model sports clubs were formed with the spreading popularity of <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> matches in 19th century Constantinople. The leading clubs, according to timeline, were <a href="/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K." title="BeÅiktaÅ J.K.">BeÅiktaÅ Gymnastics Club</a> (1903), <a href="/wiki/Galatasaray_S.K." title="Galatasaray S.K.">Galatasaray Sports Club</a> (1905), <a href="/wiki/Fenerbah%C3%A7e_S.K." title="FenerbahÃ§e S.K.">FenerbahÃ§e Sports Club</a> (1907), <a href="/wiki/MKE_Ankarag%C3%BCc%C3%BC" title="MKE AnkaragÃ¼cÃ¼">MKE AnkaragÃ¼cÃ¼ (formerly Turan SanatkaragÃ¼cÃ¼)</a> (1910) in Constantinople. Football clubs were formed in other provinces too, such as <a href="/wiki/Kar%C5%9F%C4%B1yaka_S.K." title="KarÅÄ±yaka S.K.">KarÅÄ±yaka Sports Club</a> (1912), <a href="/wiki/Altay_S.K." title="Altay S.K.">Altay Sports Club</a> (1914) and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor" title="ÃlkÃ¼spor">Turkish Fatherland Football Club</a> (later <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor" title="ÃlkÃ¼spor">ÃlkÃ¼spor</a>) (1914) of <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmir" title="Ä°zmir">Ä°zmir</a>.
</p>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Ottoman Turkish, the city was known by various names, among which were <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kostantiniyye" title="Kostantiniyye">Kostantiniyye</a></i></span> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota-Arab">ÙØ³Ø·ÙØ·ÙÙÙÙ</span></span>) (replacing the suffix <i>-polis</i> with the Arabic <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nisba_(suffix)" title="Nisba (suffix)">nisba</a></i></span>), <span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language romanization"><i lang="ota-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dersaadet" title="Dersaadet">Dersaadet</a></i></span> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota-Arab">Ø¯Ø± Ø³Ø¹Ø§Ø¯Øª</span></span>) and <i>Istanbul</i> (<span title="Ottoman Turkish (1500-1928)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ota-Arab">Ø§Ø³ØªØ§ÙØ¨ÙÙ</span></span>). Names other than <i>Istanbul</i> became obsolete in Turkish after the proclamation of the Republic of Turkey in 1923,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edhemp286_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edhemp286-5">[5]</a></sup> and after Turkey's transition to Latin script in 1928, the Turkish government in 1930 requested that foreign embassies and companies use <i>Istanbul</i>, and that name became widely accepted internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Eldem Edhem, author of an entry on Istanbul in <i>Encyclopedia of the Ottoman Empire</i>, stated that the majority of the Turkish people circa 2010, including historians, believe using "Constantinople" to refer to the Ottoman-era city is "politically incorrect" despite any historical accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edhemp286_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edhemp286-5">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The sultan from 1512 to 1520.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_VI" title="Mehmed VI">Mehmed VI</a>, the last Sultan, was expelled from Constantinople on 17 November 1922.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of SÃ¨vres">Treaty of SÃ¨vres</a> (10 August 1920) afforded a small existence to the Ottoman Empire. On 1 November 1922, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">Grand National Assembly</a> (GNAT) abolished the sultanate and declared that all the deeds of the Ottoman regime in Constantinople were null and void as of 16 March 1920, the date of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople" title="Occupation of Constantinople">occupation of Constantinople</a> under the terms of the Treaty of SÃ¨vres. The international recognition of the GNAT and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Grand_National_Assembly" title="Government of the Grand National Assembly">Government of Ankara</a> was achieved through the signing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne_(1923)" title="Treaty of Lausanne (1923)">Treaty of Lausanne</a> on 24 July 1923. The Grand National Assembly of Turkey promulgated the Republic on 29 October 1923, which ended the Ottoman Empire in history.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Sublime Ottoman State" was not used in minority languages for Christians and Jews, nor in French,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StraussConstp36_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-StraussConstp36-20">[16]</a></sup> the common Western language among the educated in the late Ottoman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StraussConstp26_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-StraussConstp26-11">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Languages of the Ottoman Empire">Minority languages, which use the same name in French</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StraussConstp36_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-StraussConstp36-20">[16]</a></sup>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottoman dynasty</a> also held the title "<a href="/wiki/List_of_caliphs#Ottoman_Caliphate_(1517_â_3_March_1924)" title="List of caliphs">caliph</a>" from the Ottoman victory over the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate" title="Mamluk Sultanate">Mamluk Sultanate of Cairo</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ridaniya" title="Battle of Ridaniya">Battle of Ridaniya</a> in 1517 to the <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Caliphate" title="Abolition of the Caliphate">abolition of the Caliphate</a> by the Turkish Republic in 1924.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The empire also temporarily gained authority over distant overseas lands through declarations of allegiance to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Dynasty" title="Ottoman Dynasty">Ottoman Sultan and Caliph</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_expedition_to_Aceh" title="Ottoman expedition to Aceh">declaration by the Sultan of Aceh</a> in 1565, or through temporary acquisitions of islands such as <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote</a> in the Atlantic Ocean in 1585.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[23]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A lock-hold on trade between western Europe and Asia is often cited as a primary motivation for <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a> to fund <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>'s westward journey to find a sailing route to Asia and, more generally, for European seafaring nations to explore alternative trade routes (e.g., K.D. Madan, <i>Life and travels of Vasco Da Gama</i> (1998), 9; I. Stavans, <i>Imagining Columbus: the literary voyage</i> (2001), 5; W.B. Wheeler and S. Becker, <i>Discovering the American Past. A Look at the Evidence: to 1877</i> (2006), 105). This traditional viewpoint has been attacked as unfounded in an influential article by A.H. Lybyer ("The Ottoman Turks and the Routes of Oriental Trade", <i>English Historical Review</i>, 120 (1915), 577â588), who sees the rise of Ottoman power and the beginnings of Portuguese and Spanish explorations as unrelated events. His view has not been universally accepted (cf. K.M. Setton, <i>The Papacy and the Levant (1204â1571), Vol. 2: The Fifteenth Century (Memoirs of the American Philosophical Society, Vol. 127)</i> (1978), 335).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though the revolt was officially initiated on the 10 June, bin Ali's sons <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_of_Hejaz" title="Ali of Hejaz">'Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">Faisal</a> had already initiated operations at Medina starting on 5 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">* <i>Learning to Read in the Late Ottoman Empire and the Early Turkish Republic</i>, B. Fortna, page 50: "Although in the late Ottoman period Persian was taught in the state schools..."
<ul><li><i>Persian Historiography and Geography</i>, Bertold Spuler, page 68: "On the whole, the circumstance in Turkey took a similar course: in Anatolia, the Persian language had played a significant role as the carrier of civilization. [...] where it was at time, to some extent, the language of diplomacy [...] However Persian maintained its position also during the early Ottoman period in the composition of histories and even Sultan Salim I, a bitter enemy of Iran and the Shi'ites, wrote poetry in Persian. Besides some poetical adaptations, the most important historiographical works are: Idris Bidlisi's flowery "Hasht Bihist", or Seven Paradises, begun in 1502 by the request of Sultan Bayazid II and covering the first eight Ottoman rulers..."</li>
<li><i>Picturing History at the Ottoman Court</i>, Emine FetvacÄ±, page 31: "Persian literature, and belles-lettres in particular, were part of the curriculum: a Persian dictionary, a manual on prose composition; and Sa'dis "Gulistan", one of the classics of Persian poetry, were borrowed. All these titles would be appropriate in the religious and cultural education of the newly converted young men."</li>
<li><i>Persian Historiography: History of Persian Literature A, Volume 10, edited by Ehsan Yarshater, Charles Melville, page 437: "Persian held a privileged place in Ottoman letters. Persian historical literature was first patronized during the reign of Mehmed II and continued unabated until the end of the 16th century."</i></li></ul>
</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDavison1964">Davison, Roderic H. (31 December 1964). <a class="external text" href="https://dx.doi.org/10.1515/9781400878765" rel="nofollow"><i>Reform in the Ottoman Empire, 1856-1876</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1515%2F9781400878765" rel="nofollow">10.1515/9781400878765</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781400878765" title="Special:BookSources/9781400878765"><bdi>9781400878765</bdi></a>. <q>There was the ruling Ottoman group, now largely concentrated in the bureaucracy centered on the Sublime Porte, and the mass of the people, mostly peasants. The efendi looked down on "the Turk," which was a term of opprobrium indicating boorishness, and preferred to think of himself as an Osmanli. His country was not Turkey, but the Ottoman State. His language was also "Ottoman"; though he might also call it "Turkish," in such a case he distinguished it from <i>kaba tÃ¼rkÃ§e</i>, or coarse Turkish, the common speech. His writing included a minimum of Turkish words, except for particles and auxiliary verbs.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Reform+in+the+Ottoman+Empire%2C+1856-1876&amp;rft.date=1964-12-31&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1515%2F9781400878765&amp;rft.isbn=9781400878765&amp;rft.aulast=Davison&amp;rft.aufirst=Roderic+H.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdx.doi.org%2F10.1515%2F9781400878765&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOttoman+Empire"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-:24-266"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:24_266-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFM._BloomS._Blair2009">M. Bloom, Jonathan; S. Blair, Sheila, eds. (2009). "Ottoman". <i>The Grove Encyclopedia of Islamic Art and Architecture</i>. Oxford University Press. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780195309911" title="Special:BookSources/9780195309911"><bdi>9780195309911</bdi></a>. <q>Throughout their history the Ottomans remained supporters of art and artists. Under their patronage a distinctive architectural style developed that combined the Islamic traditions of Anatolia, Iran and Syria with those of the Classical world and Byzantium. The result was a rationalist monumentality that favored spatial unity and architectonic expression.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Ottoman&amp;rft.btitle=The+Grove+Encyclopedia+of+Islamic+Art+and+Architecture&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=9780195309911&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOttoman+Empire"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-:2-267"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:2_267-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_267-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFreely2011">Freely 2011</a>, p.Â 35 "The mosques of the classical period are more elaborate than those of earlier times. They invented several surgical instruments that are still used today, such as forceps, catheters, scalpels, pincers and lancetsâ</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-297"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-297">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHorton1977">Horton, Paul (JulyâAugust 1977). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081122021637/http://www.saudiaramcoworld.com/issue/197704/topkapi.s.turkish.timepieces.htm" rel="nofollow">"Topkapi's Turkish Timepieces"</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saudi_Aramco_World" title="Saudi Aramco World">Saudi Aramco World</a></i>: 10â13.
